    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d5ae10c1be9148b9aa040f819bc29f27">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '15d6b3c2-6b26-4a13-a191-2e7cc2202378'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d58909659" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>NASICON-type Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>(NVP) material possesses robust 3D structure and high sodium diffusivity, thus showcasing its immense potential in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, considering the perspective of environmental conservation, it is imperative to substitute vanadium with elements that are both cost-effective and non-toxic in order to further enhance its application in SIBs. Herein, Fe is utilized to replace the V site in the sodium vanadium phosphate structure and successfully prepared a pure phase sodium-deficient NASICON (sodium superionic conductor) Na<sub>3.15</sub>VFe<sub>0.86</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVFP-650) cathode. It is found that the regulation of sintering temperature for Na<sub>3+</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>VFe(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>(NVFP) material can effectively mitigate the formation of secondary phases and enhance the electrochemical properties of the resulting product. The sodium-deficient cathode shows enhanced electrochemical performance and sodium ion diffusion kinetics. It exhibits a high capacity of 102.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, and exhibits a high-capacity retention of 95.7% after 2000 cycles at 20 C. The energy storage mechanism and structural evolution are further investigated through SEM, TEM, XPS, and in situ XRD characterizations. The compositional modulation of sodium-deficient NVFP and the elucidation of its cycling mechanisms in this work would provide valuable insights for enhancing the performance of sodium energy storage systems.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smtd202401697-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smtd202401697-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely used in various fields such as electronic products, electric vehicles and large-scale energy storage due to their high energy densities, wide operating temperature range, and decent cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0001_R_d58909649e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy">1</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0002_R_d58909649e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Science">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the increasing cost resulting from lithium resource scarcity and frequent safe issues have led to a surge in research on alternative clean energy sources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0003_R_d58909649e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Renewable Sustainable Energy Rev.">3</a>-<a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0007_R_d58909649e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries emerge as a promising candidate for rechargeable batteries as it possesses excellent electrochemical properties which are similar with LIBs but at a low price point due to the great sodium abundance on earth (ranking sixth in reserves). Moreover, SIBs surpass LIBs in terms of safety test performance. However, sodium ions possess a larger ionic radius and atomic weight than lithium ions, which will restrain the ion diffusion kinetics and energy density of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0008_R_d58909649e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a>-<a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0010_R_d58909649e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is of paramount importance to develop cost-effective cathode materials featuring stable structure with rapid sodium ion diffusion channels.</p>
         
         <p>In the past decades, researchers have made significant advancements in the study of various types of cathode materials, including layer oxide materials, polyanionic materials, and Prussian blue materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0008_R_d58909649e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0011_R_d58909649e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0015_R_d58909649e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Layered oxide materials offer high capacity but are limited by their low working voltage below 3.5&nbsp;V, which severely restricts their energy densities. Prussian blue-based materials show high capacity and cost advantages, but the content of interstitial water is difficult to control, which deteriorates its electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0015_R_d58909649e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>-<a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0018_R_d58909649e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Polyanionic materials differ from layered oxide materials as they exhibit higher operating potential and better thermal stability, making them relatively safer for practical applications as cathode materials in SIBs. Currently, the widely studied high-performance polyanion materials include Na₃V₂(PO₄)₂F₃, NaVPO₄F, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0019_R_d58909649e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a>-<a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0021_R_d58909649e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - eScience">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cao et&nbsp;al. innovatively used <i>Aspergillus niger</i> to assist in synthesizing a 3D cross-linked porous AsN@NMTP/C material and optimized the structure of NMTP cathodes by adjusting the Mn/Ti ratio.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0022_R_d58909649e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Tang groups synthesized a novel fluoro-sulfate Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F material by adjusting the NaF content in raw materials, the proportion of electrochemically active materials was optimized, providing a new idea for material property regulation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0023_R_d58909649e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among polyanionic compounds, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>with NASICON structure has garnered significant attention due to its high working voltage and exceptional thermal stability. The 3D spatial structure not only provides space for sodium ion storage but also facilitates the transport of sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0024_R_d58909649e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nanoscale">24</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0025_R_d58909649e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, the high cost and harmful environmental impact of vanadium hinder the widespread applications of NVP cathode in SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Substantial efforts have devoted to address the aforementioned issues of NVP materials. Transition metals ions partly substitution in the NVP structure were deemed as an appealing way to further regulate the structure and redox behavior of cathode material, achieving cost reduction and pollution mitigation simultaneously. A variety of transition metals have been extensively used to replace the V site, representative of Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Fe<sup>2+</sup>, Ti<sup>2+</sup>, Zr<sup>2+</sup>, Ga<sup>+</sup>, Cr<sup>2+</sup>, Nb<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0026_R_d58909649e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a>-<a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0031_R_d58909649e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nanoscale">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zhao introduce a strategy of enhancing the covalency of the NASICON framework and reversibly activating the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> couple, Na<sub>2.5</sub>VTi<sub>0.5</sub>Al<sub>0.5</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was synthesized.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0032_R_d58909649e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaFe<sub>2-x</sub>Mg<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> was synthesized through a chemo-mechanical method, with Mg doped at Fe sites in the NASICON type structure for the first time.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0033_R_d58909649e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Eng. J.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, it was found that the prices of common iron and manganese sources in the chemical industry, such as ferrous sulfate and ferric nitrate, were less than 700$ per ton. In contrast, the prices of common vanadium sources like sodium metavanadate and vanadium pentoxide vary from 1400$ to 10&nbsp;000$ per ton. The introduction of cost-effective metal elements of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup> can effectively balance the capacity and price, thereby rendering it acceptable. However, the Jahn-Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> can cause irreversible structural damage in the process of sodium insertion and sodium removal, which makes the poor cyclic stability of the material. Zhang et&nbsp;al. reported the microstructural evolution and kinetic properties during the process of iron doping. Lin's team work as equally enlightening, as it explored the multielectron redox reactions of inducing doped iron atoms in NVP materials. It providing a stronger foundation that as an inexpensive and environmentally friendly element, iron has a very good doping effect when replacing some expensive or environmentally harmful elements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0034_R_d58909649e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Alloys Compd.">34</a>-<a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0036_R_d58909649e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, NASICON-type Na<sub>4</sub>VFe(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> material can effectively circumvent this issue. Remarkably, the direct synthesis of stoichiometric Na<sub>4</sub>FeV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> via solid-state methods poses significant challenges, despite many previous attempts to prepare it.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0037_R_d58909649e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">37</a>-<a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0041_R_d58909649e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The pure phase of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sup>II</sup>V<sup>III</sup>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was not successfully obtained using solid-state and sol-gel methods in the study conducted by Park et&nbsp;al., indicating the presence of secondary phases of NaFePO<sub>4</sub>. Finally, it was achieved through the electrochemical sodiation method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0042_R_d58909649e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Carbon">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hadouchi et&nbsp;al. also made an attempt to synthesize Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sup>II</sup>V<sup>III</sup>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, but they obtained a mixed sample with sodium deficiency, Na<sub>3.41</sub>£<sub>0.59</sub>FeV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, featuring mixed valence of Fe<sup>II</sup>/Fe<sup>III</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0043_R_d58909649e679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Storage Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In this study, a series of NASICON-type NVFP materials were successfully synthesized by a straightforward sol-gel method. The obtained products are usually in a state of sodium deficiency with the mixed valence of Fe<sup>II</sup>/Fe<sup>III</sup>. The optimization of sintering temperature for NVFP material was found to effectively mitigate the formation of secondary phases and enhance the electrochemical properties of the resulting product. The sample obtained at 650 °C with no impurity phase shows enhanced reaction kinetics and cyclic stability. The NVFP-650 cathode shows a high capacity of 102.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, and exhibits capacity retention of 89.3% after 1000 cycles at a low rate of 1 C. Even at a high rate of 20 C, the NVFP-650 cathode shows a high-capacity retention of 95.7% after 2000 cycles, which is much higher than that of other sintering temperature of 600 and 700 °C (NVFP-600 and NVFP-700). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of NVFP-650 lies approximately between 1.40 × 10<sup>−8</sup> to 6.43 × 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than that of the NVFP-600 and NVFP-700 samples. The presence of heterogeneous phases will detrimentally impact the electrochemical performance of NVFP. This work presents an efficient approach for the synthesis of cost-effective NASICON-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries with superior performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smtd202401697-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smtd202401697-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The NVFP-600, NVFP-650 and NVFP-700 were synthesized via a simple sol-gel method. As illustrated in the crystal structure diagram (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0001">1a</a>), all of the NVFP materials have a typical NASICON 3D framework, where Fe/VO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and tetrahedra are interconnected through a common angle connection that facilities the sodium ion diffusion. Sodium atoms occupy two distinct sites, Na1 and Na2, with most electrochemical reactions occurring at the Na2 site.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0044_R_d58909649e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Solid State Chem.">44</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0045_R_d58909649e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Mater. Chem. A">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The doped iron atoms share positions with vanadium atoms. To better study the crystal structures at different calcination temperatures, the X-ray diffraction and the Rietveld refinement were conducted. The XRD patterns of three NVFP materials are compared in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a> (Supporting Information) and NVFP-650 sample shows a superior crystallinity compared to the other two samples.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202401697-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6c98dd0d-34cc-4768-a4e7-00909aed9e93/smtd202401697-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6c98dd0d-34cc-4768-a4e7-00909aed9e93/smtd202401697-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/37f191d8-44a8-4314-a208-65921181942f/smtd202401697-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6c98dd0d-34cc-4768-a4e7-00909aed9e93/smtd202401697-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202401697-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202401697" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of the structure of the synthetic material. a) Ball-and-stick model of NVP crystal structure. Ball-and-stick model of crystal structure of NVP doped with Fe atoms. b) Rietveld refinement of the XRD pattern of the NVFP-650 material. c) TGA curves of the NVFP-650.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Rietveld refinement results of the three samples are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0001">1b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), with the reliable factor (<i>R</i>wp) for all refinements below 3%, indicating excellent agreement between calculated and measured data. All samples fit well with the rhombohedral NASICON structure (space group <i>R</i>3<i>c</i>), with approximately 50% of V atoms at the 12c position replaced by Fe atoms. Detailed information on lattice parameters, atomic occupations, and bond lengths is provided in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S4</a> (Supporting Information). Compared to NVFP-600 and NVFP-700, the Na2─O1 and Na2─O2 bond lengths of NVFP-650 show a noticeable increase, indicating weakened binding to the Na atom at the Na2 position, thereby facilitating Na deintercalation.</p>
         
         <p>It is worth noting that only the NVFP-650 sample exhibits a pure phase. The NVFP-600 sample contains a secondary phase, NaFePO<sub>4</sub>, with a content of approximately 1.06%. while the NVFP-700 sample has three impurity phases, NaFePO₄, V₄Fe₂O₁₃ and FeP₂, with contents of 1.17%, 2.85% and 1.47%, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information). Previous studies have suggested that NaFePO<sub>4</sub> is electrochemically inert due to the lack of channels for fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in its maricite structure. Activating maricite-NaFePO<sub>4</sub> is challenging and typically requires ultra-high voltage (over 4.5&nbsp;V) constant current charging, material amorphization, or nanoization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0042_R_d58909649e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Carbon">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, an excessive NaFePO<sub>4</sub> impurity phase may negatively impact the electrochemical performance of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0042_R_d58909649e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Carbon">42</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0046_R_d58909649e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Power Sources">46</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0047_R_d58909649e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nano Energy">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, iron in Fe<sub>2</sub>P can easily dissolve in the electrolyte and migrate from the positive to the negative electrode. The formation of Fe<sub>2</sub>P is closely related to the reduction atmosphere and sintering temperature during synthesis process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0048_R_d58909649e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Energy">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is consistent with previous literature on synthetic NVFP. The presence of these impurities indicates the loss of some iron and vanadium in NVFP-600 and NVFP-700.</p>
         
         <p>To precisely determine the elemental composition of the samples, inductively coupled plasma (ICP) chemical analysis experiment was conducted, and the exact chemical formulas of the NVFP-600, NVFP-650, and NVFP-700 are Na<sub>3.19</sub>VFe<sub>0.87</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>3.16</sub>VFe<sub>0.86</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>3.18</sub>VFe<sub>0.87</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, respectively (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). It is noteworthy that all NVFP samples are in a state of sodium deficiency which is closely related to the valence state of iron. The thermal stability of the three NVFP materials was assessed through TGA analysis. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0001">1c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), the NVFP material exhibits excellent thermal stability. The TGA curve of NVFP-650 displays three distinct segments: initial weight loss due to crystal water evaporation, mass loss between 100 and 450 °C due to carbon combustion, and a subsequent mass increase after 450 °C attributed to the formation of vanadium and iron oxides upon exposure to air.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0049_R_d58909649e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Electrochim. Acta">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The morphology of NVFP-600, NVFP-650, and NVFP-700 was characterized using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0002">2a</a>, the NVFP-650 particles exhibit an irregular shape, with most particles ranging from 3 to 5&nbsp;µm in size. The particle surfaces are well-coated with a carbon layer. Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) show that NVFP-600 and NVFP-700 samples also have irregular shapes similar to NVFP-650. The well-applied carbon coating on the NVFP surfaces enhances electron conductivity. TEM revealed a well-defined crystal lattice structure in the NVFP-650 sample, the lattice fringes spaced at 0.342&nbsp;nm, 0.272&nbsp;nm and 0.243&nbsp;nm corresponding to the (006), (60-2) and (003) plane of the NASICON structure, with carbon layers 3–5&nbsp;nm in thick (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0002">2c,d</a>). Regarding the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) results, it could be seen that the diffraction spots were arranged in a regular and periodically repeating pattern. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0002">2e,f</a>, when observed under different zone axes, the symmetry of the diffraction patterns remained consistent, and no obvious impurity phases were found. EDS images confirm the uniform distribution of Na, Fe, V, and P elements in the NVFP-650 structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0002">2g</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202401697-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8e6aff38-871c-4f6a-bb86-0a912b7b781a/smtd202401697-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8e6aff38-871c-4f6a-bb86-0a912b7b781a/smtd202401697-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c44cc568-3003-4065-a382-7649a40daf84/smtd202401697-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8e6aff38-871c-4f6a-bb86-0a912b7b781a/smtd202401697-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202401697-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202401697" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustrations of the crystal structure of the NVFP-650. a) SEM image of the NVFP-650 material particle aggregate. b) The individual shaped particle SEM image of the NVFP-650 material. c) The carbon layer and lattice fringes of the NVFP-650 material with an interplanar spacing of about 0.342&nbsp;nm and 0.272&nbsp;nm corresponds to the (006) and (60-2) plane. d) The (003) crystal plane exhibits beautiful lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.243&nbsp;nm. e) Selected area electron diffraction (SEAD) pattern of NFVP-650. f) Another selected area electron diffraction (SEAD) pattern of NFVP-650 with more characteristic crystal planes. g) Elemental mapping images include Na, Fe, V, P, O, and C of the NVFP-650 material.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Then the electrochemical performance of the NVFP were evaluated though a series of electrochemical measurements. The Galvanostatic charge and discharge profiles of three NVFP samples demonstrate a favorable reversibility at a rate of 1 C (<b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), NVFP-600, NVFP-650 and NVFP-700 present two even and well-defined voltage platforms at approximately 2.5/2.6&nbsp;V and 3.5/3.4&nbsp;V, in excellent agreement with cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0003">3b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). This can be attributed to two redox couples: Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> and V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0025_R_d58909649e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0049_R_d58909649e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Electrochim. Acta">49</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0050_R_d58909649e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nano Lett.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The exceptional cycling performance of the NVFP-650 electrode was investigated in CR2032 half cells. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0003">3c</a>. The NVFP-650 electrode delivers 94.25 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial coulombic efficiency of 98.9%, and a capacity retention of 94.88% after 500 cycles at 1 C, which is comparable to the NVFP-600 but surpasses the NVFP-700. Remarkably, even after 1000 cycles, the NVFP-650 electrode maintains a capacity of 84.18 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 89.3%. Electrochemical performance tests on commercial NVP and NVFP-650 were also conducted. As can be seen from Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), for the commercial NVP at a rate of 2 C, the initial capacity was 97.12 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. After 500 cycles, the remaining capacity was 77.41 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity retention rate of 79.70%. In contrast, NVFP-650 was tested at a rate of 1 C. Its initial capacity was 94.91 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and the remaining capacity after 500 cycles was 89.42 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity retention rate of 94.21%. This excellent cycling performance is significantly superior to that of many reported phosphate materials under the same rate and number of cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0003">3f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0040_R_d58909649e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0049_R_d58909649e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Electrochim. Acta">49</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0051_R_d58909649e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">51</a>-<a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0055_R_d58909649e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NVFP-650 materials display considerable capacity of 102.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, and deliver 97.08, 91.15, 84.74, 77.05 and 67.76 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the rates of 0.5 C, 2 C, 5 C,10 C and 20 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0003">3d</a>). When it returns to 0.1 C, NVFP-650 still provided 99.98 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> which reflects the excellent structural stability. At the same current density, NVFP-600 exhibited capacity of 101.26, 99.65, 94.95, 89.57, 84.16, and 76.85 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and NVFP-700 has a capacity of 96.95, 94.58, 89.63, 84.84, 78.88, and 71.62 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The NVFP-600 demonstrates higher initial capacity at high rate than other two NVFP materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202401697-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/931360ea-8166-49d6-903c-c00597f6140f/smtd202401697-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/931360ea-8166-49d6-903c-c00597f6140f/smtd202401697-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6ed70f24-611d-487b-8840-468ae6a8a3af/smtd202401697-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/931360ea-8166-49d6-903c-c00597f6140f/smtd202401697-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202401697-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202401697" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the synthetic material. a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the NVFP-650 at 1 C (1 C = 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in this article). b) CV curves of the NVFP-650. (c) Cycle performance of NVFP-650 at 1 C. d) Rate performance of the NVFP-650 electrode at multiple rates. e) Electrochemical impedance of the pristine NVFP-650 electrodes and the NVFP-650 electrodes after 400 cycles. f) The comparison of cyclic performance of various sodium vanadium phosphate materials. g) Cycle performance of NVFP-600, the NVFP-650 and the NVFP-700 at 20 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In addition, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was employed to further analyze the charge transfer resistance of the electrode material. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0003">3e</a>, the impedance of the pristine NVFP-650 electrode decreased significantly from 883.2 to 289.15 Ω after 400 cycles, which may be related to the electrode activation process. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), the impedances of NVFP-600, NVFP-650 and NVFP-700 in the pristine state were 2323.1, 883.2, and 653.95 Ω respectively. After 400 cycles, they decreased to 876.15, 289.15, and 201.6 Ω. The electrochemical impedance of the corresponding electrode material decreases with increasing sintering temperature. The charge transfer resistance at the NVFP-650 electrode-electrolyte interface is much smaller than the NVFP-600, thereby enhancing migration kinetics.</p>
         
         <p>To further elucidate the cycling stability of the NVFP-650, it was subjected to a long-term cycling test at an elevated current density of 20 C. It delivered an impressive discharge capacity of 76.15 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Even after 2000 cycles, the NVFP-650 retained a substantial 95.7% of its initial capacity, underscoring its minimal capacity degradation and superior cyclic stability compare to other two NVFP materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0003">3g</a>). Therefore, the NVFP-650 demonstrated outstanding structural integrity and enhanced electrochemical performance. This extraordinary performance highlights the robustness and reliability of the NVFP-650 material, making it a promising candidate for high-rate and long-life energy storage applications.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The sodium ion diffusion kinetics in NVFP were comprehensively studied using cyclic voltammetry at various scan rates ranging from 0.2 to 1.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0004">4a</a>). The sodium-diffusion coefficient for the three temperatures can be calculated by the equation:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0039_R_d58909649e1018" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Chem. A">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smtd202401697-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/13daecc7-9e7f-43cb-a0ec-9263a8cb6403/smtd202401697-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:23669608:media:smtd202401697:smtd202401697-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smtd202401697-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>log</mi>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>I</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfenced>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi>log</mi>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                              <mi>a</mi>
                           </mfenced>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <mi>b</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">×</mo>
                           <mi>log</mi>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                              <mi>v</mi>
                           </mfenced>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\log \left( {I{\mathrm{p}}} \right) = \log \left( a \right) + b \times \log \left( v \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>I</i>p is the peak current, <i>v</i> is the scan rate. According to the above equation, the <i>b</i> parameter represents the coefficient related to diffusion. A <i>b</i> value of 0.5 typically indicates a diffusion-controlled process, while a <i>b</i> value of 1 suggests a capacitive process. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0004">4b,c</a>, the line fitting results show stable and reversible redox reaction in NVFP-650 structure. The oxidation and reduction peak <i>b</i> values calculated for are 0.87 and 0.69, respectively. Moreover, the b derived from the two oxidation-reduction groups are nearly identical, suggesting a high degree of reversibility in sodium ion diffusion within the structure. It is widely accepted that a <i>b</i> value of a battery material ranging from 0.5 to 1 signifies an accelerated kinetic reaction, indicating that the storage mechanism of sodium ions is governed by both diffusion and surface processes. The <i>b</i> value associated with the four identified peaks were 0.87 and 0.69, indicative of partial pesudocapative effects that contribute to exceptional rate performance and prolonged cycling stability. For another two samples, it was determined that the b values in their oxidized state as well as in their reduced state all ranged from 0.5 to 1 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information).</div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202401697-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b4e27c98-3074-479f-a552-bace8f5bcc92/smtd202401697-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b4e27c98-3074-479f-a552-bace8f5bcc92/smtd202401697-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f90f51eb-c45d-491a-8b45-1044d4e4ee0c/smtd202401697-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b4e27c98-3074-479f-a552-bace8f5bcc92/smtd202401697-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202401697-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202401697" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of the NVFP-600 at different scan rates from 0.2 to 1.2 mv s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Oxidation Log (peak current)–Log (scan rate) plots and c) reduction Log (peak current)-Log (scan rate) plots of the cathodes. d) The GITT curves and corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> chemical diffusion of NVFP-650.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was conducted for the first charge and discharge of the NVFP-650 electrode, employing a current rate of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 10 min per charge/discharge step with a resting period of 4 h between steps. The volume phase diffusion coefficient of sodium ions can also be determined using the following equation:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0056_R_d58909649e1105" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Electrochim. Acta">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smtd202401697-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/0abd878f-7dae-4f95-b3cd-0c06ae60b300/smtd202401697-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:23669608:media:smtd202401697:smtd202401697-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/smtd202401697-math-0002.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>

                                 </msup>
                              </mrow>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mfrac>
                              <mn>4</mn>

                           </mfrac>

                                       </mfrac>
                                    </mfenced>
                                 </mfenced>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfenced>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{D}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}} = \frac{4}{\Pi }{{\left( {\frac{{I{{V}_M}}}{{nFS}}} \right)}^2}{{\left[ {\left( {\frac{{{\mathrm{d}}V}}{{{\mathrm{d}}x}}} \right)\bigg/\left( {\frac{{dV}}{{d\sqrt t }}} \right)} \right]}^2}\left( {t\left\langle {\left\langle {\frac{{{{L}^2}}}{D}} \right.} \right.} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p><i>I</i> is current, represents the flow of electric charge, <i>F</i> is Faraday's constant (96485 C mol<sup>−1</sup>), n is the charge number of sodium ions, and <i>S</i> denotes the contact area between the electrode material and the electrolyte. In cases where a small applied current I and short relaxation time result in a linear relation between d<i>V</i> and d√<i>t</i>, simplification of the above equation becomes possible. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the GITT curve three NVFP materials, from which it can be observed that calculated diffusion coefficients remain consistent within the same voltage range for both the charging and discharging process, providing strong evidence for high reversibility in sodium ion insertion and removal within the structure of NVFP materials. Remarkably, the estimated range for <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of NVFP-650 lies approximately between 1.40 × 10<sup>−8</sup> and 6.43 × 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. From the charge and discharge GITT curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), it was observed that for the NVFP-650 cathode material, during the initial stage of charging, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient ranged from 2.2 to 4.0 × 10⁻⁸ cm<sup>2</sup> s⁻¹, while upon reaching the full discharge stage, the diffusion coefficient exponentially decreased to 10⁻¹⁰ cm<sup>2</sup> s⁻¹. Meanwhile, the in-situ EIS were performed in both NVFP-650 initial and full discharge states to investigate the mechanism of the Na vacancies influence. The charge transfer impedance in the full discharge state (sodium-full state) was 2425 Ω, which was higher than 887.82 Ω of the pristine (sodium-deficient) state. Reviewing the relevant works on synthesizing sodium-rich state Na<sub>4</sub>FeV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> work, the measured sodium ion diffusion coefficients fell within the ranges of 3.01 × 10⁻¹⁰ to 2.75 × 10⁻⁹ cm<sup>2</sup> s⁻¹ and 10⁻⁹ to 10⁻¹¹ cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0057_R_d58909649e1234" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">57</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0058_R_d58909649e1234" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Carbon">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the diffusion coefficient of NVFP-650 was in the range of 1.40 × 10⁻⁸ to 6.43 × 10⁻⁹ cm<sup>2</sup> s⁻¹, clearly demonstrating that the diffusion coefficients of the sodium-rich materials were lower. The above results indicate that the presence of certain sodium vacancies is conducive to the faster insertion and extraction of sodium ions during charging and discharging.</p>
         
         <p>To further understanding the sodium-ion storage mechanism of the NVFP-650 framework and to meticulously examine the valence transformations of transition metals, in-situ XRD and XPS characterization methods were used for analysis. The corresponding charging and discharging curves, as well as the in-situ XRD results, were presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0005">5a,b</a>. It can be observed that the diffraction peaks of (104), (2-13), (2-16), (030), and (4-26) located at 20.42°, 23.82°, 32.40°, 35.64°, and 48.86° under the pristine state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0037_R_d58909649e1250" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">37</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0051_R_d58909649e1250" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">51</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0058_R_d58909649e1250" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Carbon">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Throughout the galvanostatic charge-discharge process, these diffraction peaks transiently shifted towards higher angles during the initial charge, only to revert to their pristine positions upon discharge, indicative of an elastic structural response. When charged separately to 1.8, 2.4, and 2.6&nbsp;V, and there are no new diffraction peaks appear nor any significant shifts in existing diffraction peaks are observed. However, when reaching a charge of 3.4&nbsp;V, the diffraction peaks of (104), (2-13), (030), and (4-26) so on all shifted towards the higher angles which signifies the deintercalation of sodium ions. Nevertheless, when the discharge process begins, the diffraction peak shifted to the right is neatly back to their primitive Bragg positions with intensities interact, which implies a solid-solution mechanism with high structural reversibility. Furthermore, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was utilized to investigate changes in the valence states of Fe and V within the NVFP-650 materials throughout charge-discharge cycles. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0005">5c,d</a>, which depicts ex situ XPS spectra at various states of charge of the material electrodes, the pristine NVFP-650 powder exhibits peaks corresponding to trivalent iron ions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information), ferrous iron ions, and trivalent vanadium ions. When charged to 3.8&nbsp;V, the two characteristic peaks of V 2p at 524.08&nbsp;eV and 516.58&nbsp;eV correspond to trivalent vanadium ions shifted to higher binding energy positions. This reveals the transition from trivalent vanadium to tetravalent vanadium. At full charge state, two segments of spin-orbit coupling can be identified within Fe 2p region, these two peaks at the 712.18&nbsp;eV (Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 724.98&nbsp;eV (Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) binding energy positions correspond to trivalent iron ions. After full discharge, Fe 2p was detected at 709.28&nbsp;eV (Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 722.18&nbsp;eV (Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) indicating the existence of bivalent iron. However, a small amount of trivalent iron is still identified, possibly due to surface oxidation during testing. In this charge-discharge process, the alterations in binding energies for Fe and V indicate that their involvement in electron transfer processes and is in highly accordance with the electrochemical plateaus at 2.4-2.6&nbsp;V and 3.4-3.6&nbsp;V. The survey spectrum substantiated the presence of Na, V, Fe, P, O, and C. (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202401697-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/69f860f4-6bc0-4dbc-a6cf-6c7404327592/smtd202401697-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/69f860f4-6bc0-4dbc-a6cf-6c7404327592/smtd202401697-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8c870016-5a67-4eb2-a4e2-af91e555d67a/smtd202401697-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/69f860f4-6bc0-4dbc-a6cf-6c7404327592/smtd202401697-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202401697-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202401697" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of sodium storage mechanism of the NVFP-650 material. a) In situ XRD 3D patterns of NVFP-650 electrode during the initial charge and discharge process. b) Corresponding contour plot of the diffraction peaks. c) Core-level XPS spectra of Fe 2p in the fully charge state and fully charge state. d) Core-level XPS spectra of V 2p in the fully charge state and fully discharge state.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further validate the promising practical application prospects of synthesized NVFP-650 cathode materials, a full cell with NVFP-650 as the cathode, commercial hard carbon as the anode, and NaPF<sub>6</sub> as the electrolyte was assembled for electrochemical performance testing. The mass ratio of HC anode and NVFP cathode is about 1.1:1. Before assembling the full battery, a presodiation strategy was implemented for the anode in consideration of the irreversible sodium loss due to the formation of SEI. First, a half-cell (CR 2032) comprising of hard carbon electrode and Na metal was assembled and subjected to three charge–discharge cycles at a current density of 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> followed by seven cycles at a current density of 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> ending at 0.01&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0059_R_d58909649e1301" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Energy Storage Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Remove the positive electrode after it has been fully discharged.</p>
         
         <p>The voltage-capacity curve in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0006">6a</a> shows that the initial discharge capacity of hard carbon reaches 363.49 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, stabilizing around 314 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after five cycles. Following the sodium filling process, the NVFP-650 cathode and commercial hard carbon anode were assembled into a full battery with a voltage window ranging from 1.5 to 3.8&nbsp;V. The galvanostatic charge-discharge curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0006">6b,c</a>) indicate that at a current density of 0.5 C, the initial discharge capacity of 103.11 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> remains at 99.02 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even after 100 cycles, with coulombic efficiency consistently around 99%. This results in a significantly higher energy density of 296.13&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> compared to many similar electrode materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202401697-fig-0006">6d</a>), highlighting the outstanding cycling stability and substantial potential for practical applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0014_R_d58909649e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0026_R_d58909649e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0045_R_d58909649e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Mater. Chem. A">45</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0058_R_d58909649e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Carbon">58</a>, <a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0060_R_d58909649e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Russ. J. Electrochem.">60</a>-<a href="#smtd202401697-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202401697-bib-0063_R_d58909649e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202401697-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/40141f67-f26b-469c-b487-881fc412d274/smtd202401697-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/40141f67-f26b-469c-b487-881fc412d274/smtd202401697-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9fb228cd-6aee-4bd8-ab7a-5741f8ecf454/smtd202401697-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/40141f67-f26b-469c-b487-881fc412d274/smtd202401697-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202401697-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202401697" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of HC/Na half cell. b) The galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of NVFP-650/HC full cell. c) The cycle performance of NVFP-650/HC full cell at 1C. d) Comparison between the NVFP-650 and diverse positive electrode materials reported in the aspects of energy density, and specific capacity.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smtd202401697-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smtd202401697-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the Na<sub>3.15</sub>VFe<sub>0.86</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> sample was synthesized via a straightforward sol-gel method, demonstrating remarkable performance as a cathode material for SIBs. No other heterophase was detected in NVFP-650, which contributes to the enhanced structural stability of NVFP. Moreover, the presence of sodium vacancies is beneficial for the kinetics of sodium ion migration. The modified material exhibited favorable electrochemical performance and high cycling stability. The NVFP-650 electrode delivers 102.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and it also maintained excellent performance under high current density conditions, retaining 95.7% of its initial capacity after 2000 cycles. In situ XRD analysis confirmed the highly stable of structure during cycling. The result of GITT test indicated that the diffusion coefficient of sodium ions in the sodium-deficient cathode ranged from approximately 1.4 × 10<sup>−8</sup> to 6.43 × 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is a significant enhancement compared to other materials. The stable structure and outstanding cyclic performance of NVFP-650 render it a highly promising candidate for use as a cathode material in SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smtd202401697-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smtd202401697-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smtd202401697-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smtd202401697-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smtd202401697-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smtd202401697-sec-0070-title"> Material Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>NVFP-650 was synthesized using simple sol-gel methods, employing raw reagents of high analytical purity. Initially, solution A was prepared by dissolving 2&nbsp;mmol of ferrous acetate and 0.28&nbsp;g of citric acid (acting as a reducing and chelating agent) in 30&nbsp;mL of distilled water under continuous magnetic stirring for 30 min. Subsequently, solution B was formed by adding a stoichiometric amount of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (1 mmol), NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (6 mmol), NaCH<sub>3</sub>COO (8 mmol), and oxalic acid to 30&nbsp;mL of distilled water. Solution B was then slowly added dropwise to solution A while ensuring thorough mixing. The prepared solution was heated overnight at 80 °C to form a gel-like precursor material. Finally, the solid precursor underwent fine grinding before being pre-heated at 350 °C for 4 hours and subsequently annealed at 650 °C for 12 h under steady pure N<sub>2</sub> flow to obtain the NVFP-650 material. Similarly, NVFP-600 sample and NVFP-700 were synthesized following the same procedure but with different annealing temperatures.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smtd202401697-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smtd202401697-sec-0080-title"> Electrochemical Test</h4>
                  
                  <p>The electrochemical properties of the obtained materials were evaluated using CR 2032 coin cells. The NVFP electrodes consisted of active materials, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) in a mass ratio of 7:2:1 to form an electrode slurry with an appropriate amount of N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) as a solvent. Commercial hard carbon, acetylene black, CMC and SBR (binder), and water are mixed evenly into polar sheet slurry in the ratio of 8:1:1. The resulting slurry was coated onto aluminum foil and shaped into 10&nbsp;mm discs. The active mass loading of the NVFP cathodes was about 1.016&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Before battery assembly, the electrodes were dried overnight in a 110 °C oven. For cell assembly, Na metal served as the counter electrode, while a 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte solution (90&nbsp;µL) and glass fiber separator (Whatman, GF/A) were utilized. All battery assembly procedures were conducted inside a glove box filled with pure Ar gas and maintained free from moisture content. Galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique tests were performed by a NEWARE battery testing system. Cyclic voltammetry and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analyses were carried out utilizing a CHI660D electrochemical workstation manufactured by Chenhua Instrument Company in Shanghai, China, and the impedance test frequencies span from 100&nbsp;kHz to 10 mHz.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smtd202401697-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smtd202401697-sec-0090-title"> Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The crystal structure of the NVFP materials was characterized using a Bruker D8 advance diffractometer equipped with Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 1.5416 Å). The X-ray diffraction data was collected within the 2<i>θ</i> angle range from 10° to 80°. Rietveld refinement method, implemented in the Fullprof program, was employed for structural analysis. Furthermore, precise quantitative elemental analysis was obtained using an Agilent ICP-OES spectrometer. The thermal stability measurements were conducted under ambient air atmosphere using a simultaneous thermal analyzer (HITACHI STA200) with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup>, ranging from room temperature to 600 °C. The microstructure and morphology of NVFP-600, NVFP-650, and NVFP-700 were observed by using a scanning electron microscope (SEM, ZEISS Supra 55). The information about the lattice structure, crystal plane stripes and pure phase of NVFP-650 was observed transmission electron microscope (TEM, JEM–3200FS). The changes in the valence state of elements during charge and discharge states were examined through X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Fisher ESCALAB 250Xi) employing an Al Kα source.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
